<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Dream</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Event occurring in the mind while sleeping</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Dream_(disambiguation)" title="Dream (disambiguation)">Dream (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Briullov,_Karl_-_A_Dream_of_a_Girl_Before_a_Sunrise.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="624" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="226" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Briullov%2C_Karl_-_A_Dream_of_a_Girl_Before_a_Sunrise.jpg/290px-Briullov%2C_Karl_-_A_Dream_of_a_Girl_Before_a_Sunrise.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Briullov%2C_Karl_-_A_Dream_of_a_Girl_Before_a_Sunrise.jpg/435px-Briullov%2C_Karl_-_A_Dream_of_a_Girl_Before_a_Sunrise.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Briullov%2C_Karl_-_A_Dream_of_a_Girl_Before_a_Sunrise.jpg/580px-Briullov%2C_Karl_-_A_Dream_of_a_Girl_Before_a_Sunrise.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Briullov,_Karl_-_A_Dream_of_a_Girl_Before_a_Sunrise.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>A Dream of a Girl Before a Sunrise</i> c. 1830–33 by <a href="/wiki/Karl_Bryullov" title="Karl Bryullov">Karl Bryullov</a> (1799–1852)</div></div></div>
<p>A <b>dream</b> is a succession of <a href="/wiki/Image" title="Image">images</a>, <a href="/wiki/Idea" title="Idea">ideas</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emotions" title="Emotions">emotions</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sensation_(psychology)" title="Sensation (psychology)">sensations</a> that usually occur involuntarily in the <a href="/wiki/Mind" title="Mind">mind</a> during certain stages of <a href="/wiki/Sleep" title="Sleep">sleep</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> Humans spend about two hours dreaming per night,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> and each dream lasts around 5 to 20 minutes, although the dreamer may perceive the dream as being much longer than this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HSWDream_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-HSWDream-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>The content and function of dreams have been topics of scientific, philosophical and religious interest throughout <a href="/wiki/Recorded_history" title="Recorded history">recorded history</a>. <a href="/wiki/Dream_interpretation" title="Dream interpretation">Dream interpretation</a>, practiced by the <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonians</a> in the third millennium BCE<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> and even earlier by the ancient <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerians</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BlackGreen1992_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-BlackGreen1992-6">[6]</a></sup> figures prominently in religious texts in several traditions, and has played a lead role in psychotherapy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freud1_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Freud1-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> The scientific study of dreams is called <a href="/wiki/Oneirology" title="Oneirology">oneirology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Most modern dream study focuses on the neurophysiology of dreams and on proposing and testing hypotheses regarding dream function. It is not known where in the brain dreams originate, if there is a single origin for dreams or if multiple regions of the brain are involved, or what the purpose of dreaming is for the body or mind.
</p><p>The human dream experience and what to make of it has undergone sizable shifts over the course of history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dodds1_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dodds1-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Packer1_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Packer1-11">[11]</a></sup> Long ago, according to writings from <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a>, dreams dictated post-dream behaviors to an extent sharply reduced in later millennia. These ancient writings about dreams highlight visitation dreams, where a dream figure, usually a deity or a prominent forebear, commands the dreamer to take specific actions and may predict future events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krippner1_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Krippner1-14">[14]</a></sup> The brain activity capable of formulating such dreams, rare among literate people in later eras, conforms to the <a href="/wiki/Bicameral_mentality" title="Bicameral mentality">bicameral mentality</a> hypothesized by <a href="/wiki/Julian_Jaynes" title="Julian Jaynes">Julian Jaynes</a> as dominant into the second or first millennium BCE.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This is an unsourced assertion. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Framing the dream experience varies across cultures as well as through time.
</p><p>Dreaming and sleep are intertwined. Dreams occur mainly in the <a href="/wiki/Rapid_eye_movement_sleep" title="Rapid eye movement sleep">rapid-eye movement (REM) stage of sleep</a>—when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brain_activity" title="Brain activity">brain activity</a> is high and resembles that of being awake. Because REM sleep is detectable in many species, and because research suggests that all mammals experience REM,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> linking dreams to REM sleep has led to conjectures that animals dream. However, humans dream during non-REM sleep, also, and not all REM awakenings elicit dream reports.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Solms1_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Solms1-16">[16]</a></sup> To be studied, a dream must first be reduced to a verbal report, which is an account of the subject's memory of the dream, not the subject's dream experience itself. So, dreaming by non-humans is currently unprovable, as is dreaming by human fetuses and pre-verbal infants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Subjective_experience">Subjective experience</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Oneiromancy" title="Oneiromancy">Oneiromancy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Raja_Ravi_Varma,_Usha%27s_Dream_(oleographic_print).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="488" data-file-width="805" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Raja_Ravi_Varma%2C_Usha%27s_Dream_%28oleographic_print%29.jpg/250px-Raja_Ravi_Varma%2C_Usha%27s_Dream_%28oleographic_print%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Raja_Ravi_Varma%2C_Usha%27s_Dream_%28oleographic_print%29.jpg/375px-Raja_Ravi_Varma%2C_Usha%27s_Dream_%28oleographic_print%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Raja_Ravi_Varma%2C_Usha%27s_Dream_%28oleographic_print%29.jpg/500px-Raja_Ravi_Varma%2C_Usha%27s_Dream_%28oleographic_print%29.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Raja_Ravi_Varma,_Usha%27s_Dream_(oleographic_print).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Usha Dreaming Aniruddha</i> (oleographic print) <a href="/wiki/Raja_Ravi_Varma" title="Raja Ravi Varma">Raja Ravi Varma</a> (1848–1906)</div></div></div>
<p>Preserved writings from early Mediterranean civilizations indicate a relatively abrupt change in subjective dream experience between <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> antiquity and the beginnings of the <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical era</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>In visitation dreams reported in ancient writings, dreamers were largely passive in their dreams, and visual content served primarily to frame authoritative auditory messaging.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dodds1_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dodds1-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gudea" title="Gudea">Gudea</a>, the king of the Sumerian city-state of <a href="/wiki/Lagash" title="Lagash">Lagash</a> (reigned <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 2144–2124 BCE), rebuilt the temple of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ningirsu" title="Ningirsu">Ningirsu</a> as the result of a dream in which he was told to do so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BlackGreen1992_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-BlackGreen1992-6">[6]</a></sup> After antiquity, the passive hearing of visitation dreams largely gave way to visualized narratives in which the dreamer becomes a character who actively participates.
</p><p>From the 1940s to 1985, <a href="/wiki/Calvin_S._Hall" title="Calvin S. Hall">Calvin S. Hall</a> collected more than 50,000 dream reports at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Reserve_University" title="Western Reserve University">Western Reserve University</a>. In 1966, Hall and Robert Van de Castle published <i>The Content Analysis of Dreams</i>, in which they outlined a coding system to study 1,000 dream reports from college students.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hallcontent_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-hallcontent-21">[21]</a></sup> Results indicated that participants from varying parts of the world demonstrated similarity in their dream content. The only residue of antiquity's authoritative dream figure in the Hall and Van de Castle listing of dream characters is the inclusion of God in the category of prominent persons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Hall's complete dream reports were made publicly available in the mid-1990s by his protégé <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Domhoff" title="William Domhoff">William Domhoff</a>. More recent studies of dream reports, while providing more detail, continue to cite the Hall study favorably.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dream_of_Polish_volunteers_in_French_army_1914.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1960" data-file-width="1532" decoding="async" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Dream_of_Polish_volunteers_in_French_army_1914.JPG/200px-Dream_of_Polish_volunteers_in_French_army_1914.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Dream_of_Polish_volunteers_in_French_army_1914.JPG/300px-Dream_of_Polish_volunteers_in_French_army_1914.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Dream_of_Polish_volunteers_in_French_army_1914.JPG/400px-Dream_of_Polish_volunteers_in_French_army_1914.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dream_of_Polish_volunteers_in_French_army_1914.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A soldier dreams: the trenches of WWI. <a href="/wiki/Jan_Styka" title="Jan Styka">Jan Styka</a> (1858–1925).</div></div></div>
<p>In the Hall study, the most common emotion experienced in dreams was <a href="/wiki/Anxiety" title="Anxiety">anxiety</a>. Other emotions included <a href="/wiki/Abandonment_(emotional)" title="Abandonment (emotional)">abandonment</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anger" title="Anger">anger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fear" title="Fear">fear</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joy" title="Joy">joy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Happiness" title="Happiness">happiness</a>. Negative emotions were much more common than positive ones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hallcontent_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-hallcontent-21">[21]</a></sup> The Hall data analysis showed that sexual dreams occur no more than 10% of the time and are more prevalent in young to mid-teens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hallcontent_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-hallcontent-21">[21]</a></sup> Another study showed that 8% of both men's and women's dreams have sexual content.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> In some cases, sexual dreams may result in <a href="/wiki/Orgasm" title="Orgasm">orgasms</a> or <a href="/wiki/Nocturnal_emission" title="Nocturnal emission">nocturnal emissions</a>. These are colloquially known as "wet dreams".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>The visual nature of dreams is generally highly phantasmagoric; that is, different locations and objects continuously blend into each other. The visuals (including locations, people, and objects) are generally reflective of a person's memories and experiences, but conversation can take on highly exaggerated and bizarre forms. Some dreams may even tell elaborate stories wherein the dreamer enters entirely new, complex worlds and awakes with ideas, thoughts and feelings never experienced prior to the dream.
</p><p>People who are blind from birth do not have visual dreams. Their dream contents are related to other senses like <a href="/wiki/Hearing" title="Hearing">hearing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Somatosensory_system" title="Somatosensory system">touch</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olfaction" title="Olfaction">smell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taste" title="Taste">taste</a>, whichever are present since birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Neurophysiology">Neurophysiology</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_neuroscience_of_dreams" title="Cognitive neuroscience of dreams">Cognitive neuroscience of dreams</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Neuroscience_of_sleep" title="Neuroscience of sleep">Neuroscience of sleep</a></div>
<p>Dream study is popular with scientists exploring the <a href="/wiki/Mind%E2%80%93body_problem" title="Mind–body problem">mind–brain problem</a>. Some "propose to reduce aspects of dream phenomenology to neurobiology."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> But current science cannot specify dream physiology in detail. Protocols in most nations restrict human brain research to non-invasive procedures. In the United States, invasive brain procedures with a human subject are allowed only when these are deemed necessary in surgical treatment to address medical needs of the same human subject.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Non-invasive measures of brain activity like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electroencephalogram" title="Electroencephalogram">electroencephalogram</a> (EEG) voltage averaging or <a href="/wiki/Functional_magnetic_resonance_imaging" title="Functional magnetic resonance imaging">cerebral blood flow</a> cannot identify small but influential neuronal populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hob_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-hob-29">[29]</a></sup> Also, <a href="/wiki/Functional_magnetic_resonance_imaging" title="Functional magnetic resonance imaging">fMRI</a> signals are too slow to explain how brains compute in real time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>Scientists researching some brain functions can work around current restrictions by examining animal subjects. As stated by the <a href="/wiki/Society_for_Neuroscience" title="Society for Neuroscience">Society for Neuroscience</a>, "Because no adequate alternatives exist, much of this research must be done on animal subjects."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> However, since animal dreaming can be only inferred, not confirmed, animal studies yield no hard facts to illuminate the neurophysiology of dreams. Examining human subjects with brain lesions can provide clues, but the lesion method cannot discriminate between the effects of destruction and disconnection and cannot target specific neuronal groups in heterogeneous regions like the brain stem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hob_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-hob-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Generation">Generation</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antonio_de_Pereda_-_El_sue%C3%B1o_del_caballero_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2501" data-file-width="3535" decoding="async" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Antonio_de_Pereda_-_El_sue%C3%B1o_del_caballero_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/250px-Antonio_de_Pereda_-_El_sue%C3%B1o_del_caballero_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Antonio_de_Pereda_-_El_sue%C3%B1o_del_caballero_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/375px-Antonio_de_Pereda_-_El_sue%C3%B1o_del_caballero_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Antonio_de_Pereda_-_El_sue%C3%B1o_del_caballero_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/500px-Antonio_de_Pereda_-_El_sue%C3%B1o_del_caballero_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Antonio_de_Pereda_-_El_sue%C3%B1o_del_caballero_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/The_Gentleman%27s_Dream" title="The Gentleman's Dream">The Knight's Dream</a></i>, 1655, by <a href="/wiki/Antonio_de_Pereda" title="Antonio de Pereda">Antonio de Pereda</a></div></div></div>
<p>Denied precision tools, obliged to depend on imaging, much dream research has succumbed to the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_the_instrument" title="Law of the instrument">law of the instrument</a>. Studies detect an increase of blood flow in a specific brain region and then credit that region with a role in generating dreams. But pooling study results has led to the newer conclusion that dreaming involves large numbers of regions and pathways, which likely are different for different dream events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>Image creation in the brain involves significant neural activity downstream from eye intake, and it is theorized that "the visual imagery of dreams is produced by activation during sleep of the same structures that generate complex visual imagery in waking perception."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>Dreams present a running narrative rather than exclusively visual imagery. Following their work with <a href="/wiki/Split-brain" title="Split-brain">split-brain</a> subjects, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Gazzaniga" title="Michael Gazzaniga">Gazzaniga</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_E._LeDoux" title="Joseph E. LeDoux">LeDoux</a> postulated, without attempting to specify the neural mechanisms, a "<a href="/wiki/Left-brain_interpreter" title="Left-brain interpreter">left-brain interpreter</a>" that seeks to create a plausible narrative from whatever electro-chemical signals reach the brain's left hemisphere. Sleep research has determined that some brain regions fully active during waking are, during REM sleep, activated only in a partial or fragmentary way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Drawing on this knowledge, textbook author James W. Kalat explains, "[A] dream represents the brain's effort to make sense of sparse and distorted information.... The cortex combines this haphazard input with whatever other activity was already occurring and does its best to synthesize a story that makes sense of the information."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Neuroscientist <a href="/wiki/Indre_Viskontas" title="Indre Viskontas">Indre Viskontas</a> is even more blunt, calling often bizarre dream content "just the result of your interpreter trying to create a story out of random neural signaling."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theories_on_function">Theories on function</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Oneirology" title="Oneirology">Oneirology</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Rapid_eye_movement_sleep" title="Rapid eye movement sleep">Rapid eye movement sleep</a></div>
<p>For humans in the pre-classical era, and continuing for some non-literate populations into modern times, dreams are believed to have functioned as revealers of truths sourced during sleep from gods or other external entities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Packer1_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Packer1-11">[11]</a></sup> Ancient Egyptians believed that dreams were the best way to receive divine revelation, and thus they would induce (or "incubate") dreams. They went to sanctuaries and slept on special "dream beds" in hope of receiving advice, comfort, or healing from the gods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krippner1_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Krippner1-14">[14]</a></sup> From a Darwinian perspective dreams would have to fulfill some kind of biological requirement, provide some benefit for natural selection to take place, or at least have no negative impact on fitness. Robert (1886),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> a physician from Hamburg, was the first who suggested that dreams are a need and that they have the function to erase (a) sensory impressions that were not fully worked up, and (b) ideas that were not fully developed during the day. In dreams, incomplete material is either removed (suppressed) or deepened and included into memory. <a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">Freud</a>, whose dream studies focused on interpreting dreams, not explaining how or why humans dream, disputed Robert's hypothesis<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> and proposed that dreams preserve sleep by representing as fulfilled those wishes that otherwise would awaken the dreamer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Freud wrote that dreams "serve the purpose of prolonging sleep instead of waking up. <i>Dreams are the</i> GUARDIANS <i>of sleep and not its disturbers.</i>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Taras_Shevchenko_painting_0019.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2153" data-file-width="2504" decoding="async" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Taras_Shevchenko_painting_0019.jpg/250px-Taras_Shevchenko_painting_0019.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Taras_Shevchenko_painting_0019.jpg/375px-Taras_Shevchenko_painting_0019.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Taras_Shevchenko_painting_0019.jpg/500px-Taras_Shevchenko_painting_0019.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Taras_Shevchenko_painting_0019.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Grandmother and Granddaughter Dream</i> (1839 or 1840). <a href="/wiki/Taras_Shevchenko" title="Taras Shevchenko">Taras Shevchenko</a></div></div></div>
<p>A turning point in theorizing about dream function came in 1953, when <a href="/wiki/Science_(journal)" title="Science (journal)"><i>Science</i></a> published the <a href="/wiki/Eugene_Aserinsky" title="Eugene Aserinsky">Aserinsky</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nathaniel_Kleitman" title="Nathaniel Kleitman">Kleitman</a> paper<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> establishing <a href="/wiki/Rapid_eye_movement_sleep" title="Rapid eye movement sleep">REM sleep</a> as a distinct phase of sleep and linking dreams to REM sleep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Until and even after publication of the Solms 2000 paper that certified the separability of REM sleep and dream phenomena,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Solms1_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Solms1-16">[16]</a></sup> many studies purporting to uncover the function of dreams have in fact been studying not dreams but measurable REM sleep.
</p><p>Theories of dream function since the identification of REM sleep include:
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Allan_Hobson" title="Allan Hobson">Hobson's</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_McCarley" title="Robert McCarley">McCarley's</a> 1977 <a href="/wiki/Activation-synthesis_hypothesis" title="Activation-synthesis hypothesis">activation-synthesis hypothesis</a>, which proposed "a functional role for dreaming sleep in promoting some aspect of the learning process...."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> In 2010 a Harvard study was published showing experimental evidence that dreams were correlated with improved learning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Francis_Crick" title="Francis Crick">Crick's</a> and Mitchison's 1983 "<a href="/wiki/Reverse_learning" title="Reverse learning">reverse learning</a>" theory, which states that dreams are like the cleaning-up operations of computers when they are offline, removing (suppressing) parasitic nodes and other "junk" from the mind during sleep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ernest_Hartmann" title="Ernest Hartmann">Hartmann's</a> 1995 proposal that dreams serve a "quasi-therapeutic" function, enabling the dreamer to process trauma in a safe place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Antti_Revonsuo" title="Antti Revonsuo">Revonsuo's</a> 2000 threat simulation hypothesis, whose premise is that during much of human evolution, physical and interpersonal threats were serious, giving reproductive advantage to those who survived them. Dreaming aided survival by replicating these threats and providing the dreamer with practice in dealing with them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/David_Eagleman" title="David Eagleman">Eagleman's</a> and <a href="/wiki/Don_Vaughn" title="Don Vaughn">Vaughn's</a> 2021 defensive activation theory, which says that, given the brain's <a href="/wiki/Neuroplasticity" title="Neuroplasticity">neuroplasticity</a>, dreams evolved as a visual hallucinatory activity during sleep's extended periods of darkness, busying the occipital lobe and thereby protecting it from possible appropriation by other, non-vision, sense operations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_and_other_cultural_contexts">Religious and other cultural contexts</span></h2><p>
Dreams figure prominently in major world religions. The dream experience for early humans, according to one interpretation, gave rise to the notion of a human "<a href="/wiki/Soul#Religious_views" title="Soul">soul</a>,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> a central element in much religious thought. <a href="/wiki/J._W._Dunne" title="J. W. Dunne">J. W. Dunne</a> wrote: </p><blockquote><p>But there can be no reasonable doubt that the idea of a soul must have first arisen in the mind of primitive man as a result of observation of his dreams. Ignorant as he was, he could have come to no other conclusion but that, in dreams, he left his sleeping body in one universe and went wandering off into another. It is considered that, but for that savage, the idea of such a thing as a 'soul' would never have even occurred to mankind....<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hindu">Hindu</span></h3>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Mandukya_Upanishad" title="Mandukya Upanishad">Mandukya Upanishad</a>, part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Veda" title="Veda">Veda</a> scriptures of <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Indian Hinduism</a>, a dream is one of three states that the soul experiences during its lifetime, the other two states being the waking state and the sleep state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> The earliest <a href="/wiki/Upanishads" title="Upanishads">Upanishads</a>, written before 300 BCE, emphasize two meanings of dreams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> The first says that dreams are merely expressions of inner desires. The second is the belief of the soul leaving the body and being guided until awakened.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Abrahamic">Abrahamic</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Michael_Lukas_Leopold_Willmann_001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1762" data-file-width="2024" decoding="async" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Michael_Lukas_Leopold_Willmann_001.jpg/250px-Michael_Lukas_Leopold_Willmann_001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Michael_Lukas_Leopold_Willmann_001.jpg/375px-Michael_Lukas_Leopold_Willmann_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Michael_Lukas_Leopold_Willmann_001.jpg/500px-Michael_Lukas_Leopold_Willmann_001.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Michael_Lukas_Leopold_Willmann_001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jacob's dream of a <a href="/wiki/Jacob%27s_Ladder" title="Jacob's Ladder">ladder of angels</a>, c. 1690. <a href="/wiki/Michael_Willmann" title="Michael Willmann">Michael Willmann</a></div></div></div>
<p>In Judaism, dreams are considered part of the experience of the world that can be interpreted and from which lessons can be garnered. It is discussed in the Talmud, Tractate Berachot 55–60.
</p><p>The ancient <a href="/wiki/Hebrews" title="Hebrews">Hebrews</a> connected their dreams heavily with their religion, though the Hebrews were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monotheistic" title="Monotheistic">monotheistic</a> and believed that dreams were the voice of one God alone. Hebrews also differentiated between good dreams (from God) and bad dreams (from evil spirits). The Hebrews, like many other ancient cultures, incubated dreams in order to receive a divine revelation. For example, the Hebrew prophet <a href="/wiki/Samuel" title="Samuel">Samuel</a> would "lie down and sleep in the temple at <a href="/wiki/Shiloh_(biblical_city)" title="Shiloh (biblical city)">Shiloh</a> before the Ark and receive the word of the Lord". Most of the dreams in the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a> are in the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Genesis" title="Book of Genesis">Book of Genesis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christians</a> mostly shared the beliefs of the Hebrews and thought that dreams were of a supernatural character because the <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a> includes frequent stories of dreams with divine inspiration. The most famous of these dream stories was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jacob%27s_ladder" title="Jacob's ladder">Jacob's dream of a ladder</a> that stretches from Earth to <a href="/wiki/Heaven" title="Heaven">Heaven</a>. Many Christians preach that God can speak to people through their dreams. The famous glossary, the <a href="/wiki/Somniale_Danielis" title="Somniale Danielis">Somniale Danielis</a>, written in the name of <a href="/wiki/Daniel_(biblical_figure)" title="Daniel (biblical figure)">Daniel</a>, attempted to teach Christian populations to interpret their dreams.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Iain_R._Edgar" title="Iain R. Edgar">Iain R. Edgar</a> has researched the role of dreams in <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> He has argued that dreams play an important role in the history of Islam and the lives of Muslims, since dream interpretation is the only way that Muslims can receive revelations from God since the death of the last prophet, <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iain_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Iain-57">[57]</a></sup> According to Edgar, Islam classifies three types of dreams. Firstly, there is the true dream (al-ru’ya), then the false dream, which may come from the devil (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shaytan" title="Shaytan">shaytan</a>), and finally, the meaningless everyday dream (hulm). This last dream could be brought forth by the dreamer's ego or base appetite based on what they experienced in the real world. The true dream is often indicated by Islam's <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadith</a> tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iain_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Iain-57">[57]</a></sup> In one narration by <a href="/wiki/Aisha" title="Aisha">Aisha</a>, the wife of the Prophet, it is said that the Prophet's dreams would come true like the ocean's waves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iain_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-Iain-57">[57]</a></sup> Just as in its predecessors, the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a> also recounts the story of Joseph and his unique ability to interpret dreams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iain_57-3"><a href="#cite_note-Iain-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Buddhist">Buddhist</span></h3>
<p>In Buddhism, ideas about dreams are similar to the classical and folk traditions in South Asia. The same dream is sometimes experienced by multiple people, as in the case of the <a href="/wiki/Bodhisattva#Theravada_Buddhism" title="Bodhisattva">Buddha-to-be</a>, before he is <a href="/wiki/Great_Renunciation" title="Great Renunciation">leaving his home</a>. It is described in the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mahavastu" title="Mahavastu">Mahāvastu</a></i> that several of the Buddha's relatives had premonitory dreams preceding this. Some dreams are also seen to transcend time: the Buddha-to-be has certain dreams that are the same as those of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_the_named_Buddhas" title="List of the named Buddhas">previous Buddhas</a>, the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lalitavistara" title="Lalitavistara">Lalitavistara</a></i> states. In Buddhist literature, dreams often function as a "signpost" motif to mark certain stages in the life of the main character.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Young_2003_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Young_2003-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>Buddhist views about dreams are expressed in the <a href="/wiki/Atthakatha" title="Atthakatha">Pāli Commentaries</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Milinda_Panha" title="Milinda Panha">Milinda Pañhā</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Young_2003_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Young_2003-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hupao.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" decoding="async" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Hupao.jpg/300px-Hupao.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Hupao.jpg/450px-Hupao.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Hupao.jpg/600px-Hupao.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hupao.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Dreaming_of_the_Tiger_Spring" title="Dreaming of the Tiger Spring">Dreaming of the Tiger Spring</a> (虎跑夢泉) Statue at Hupao Spring (Hupaomengquan) in Hangzhou, Zhejiang, China.</div></div></div>
<p>In Chinese history, people wrote of two vital aspects of the soul of which one is freed from the body during slumber to journey in a dream realm, while the other remained in the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bulkeley-71_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-bulkeley-71-59">[59]</a></sup> This belief and dream interpretation had been questioned since early times, such as by the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Wang_Chong" title="Wang Chong">Wang Chong</a> (27–97 <span title="Common Era">CE</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bulkeley-71_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-bulkeley-71-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Babylonians and Assyrians divided dreams into "good," which were sent by the gods, and "bad," sent by demons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> A surviving collection of dream omens entitled <i><a href="/wiki/I%C5%A1kar_Zaq%C4%ABqu" title="Iškar Zaqīqu">Iškar Zaqīqu</a></i> records various dream scenarios as well as <a href="/wiki/Prognosis" title="Prognosis">prognostications</a> of what will happen to the person who experiences each dream, apparently based on previous cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BlackGreen1992_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-BlackGreen1992-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Some list different possible outcomes, based on occasions in which people experienced similar dreams with different results.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BlackGreen1992_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-BlackGreen1992-6">[6]</a></sup> The Greeks shared their beliefs with the Egyptians on how to interpret good and bad dreams, and the idea of incubating dreams. <a href="/wiki/Morpheus" title="Morpheus">Morpheus</a>, the Greek god of dreams, also sent warnings and prophecies to those who slept at shrines and temples. The earliest Greek beliefs about dreams were that their gods physically visited the dreamers, where they entered through a keyhole, exiting the same way after the divine message was given.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Antiphon_(orator)" title="Antiphon (orator)">Antiphon</a> wrote the first known Greek book on dreams in the 5th century BCE. In that century, other cultures influenced Greeks to develop the belief that souls left the sleeping body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hippocrates" title="Hippocrates">Hippocrates</a> (469–399 <span title="Common Era">BCE</span>) had a simple dream theory: during the day, the soul receives images; during the night, it produces images. Greek philosopher <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> (384–322 BCE) believed dreams caused <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physiological" title="Physiological">physiological</a> activity. He thought dreams could analyze illness and predict diseases. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marcus_Tullius_Cicero" title="Marcus Tullius Cicero">Marcus Tullius Cicero</a>, for his part, believed that all dreams are produced by thoughts and conversations a dreamer had during the preceding days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Cicero's <i><a href="/wiki/Somnium_Scipionis" title="Somnium Scipionis">Somnium Scipionis</a></i> described a lengthy dream vision, which in turn was commented on by <a href="/wiki/Macrobius" title="Macrobius">Macrobius</a> in his <i>Commentarii in Somnium Scipionis</i>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> in his <i><a href="/wiki/Histories_(Herodotus)" title="Histories (Herodotus)">The Histories</a></i>, writes "The visions that occur to us in dreams are, more often than not, the things we have been concerned about during the day."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Dreaming_(Australian_Aboriginal_art)" title="Dreaming (Australian Aboriginal art)">The Dreaming</a> is a common term within the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animist" title="Animist">animist</a> creation narrative of <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_Australians" title="Indigenous Australians">indigenous Australians</a> for a personal, or group, <a href="/wiki/Creation_myth" title="Creation myth">creation</a> and for what may be understood as the "timeless time" of formative creation and perpetual creating.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Indigenous American</a> tribes and <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexican</a> populations believe that dreams are a way of visiting and having contact with their <a href="/wiki/Ancestor" title="Ancestor">ancestors</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Some <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Native American</a> tribes have used <a href="/wiki/Vision_quest" title="Vision quest">vision quests</a> as a rite of passage, fasting and praying until an anticipated guiding dream was received, to be shared with the rest of the tribe upon their return.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Interpretation">Interpretation</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dream_interpretation" title="Dream interpretation">Dream interpretation</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Psychoanalysis" title="Psychoanalysis">Psychoanalysis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Precognition" title="Precognition">Precognition</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tissot_Joseph_Interprets_Pharaoh%27s_Dream.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2002" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Tissot_Joseph_Interprets_Pharaoh%27s_Dream.jpg/250px-Tissot_Joseph_Interprets_Pharaoh%27s_Dream.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Tissot_Joseph_Interprets_Pharaoh%27s_Dream.jpg/375px-Tissot_Joseph_Interprets_Pharaoh%27s_Dream.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Tissot_Joseph_Interprets_Pharaoh%27s_Dream.jpg/500px-Tissot_Joseph_Interprets_Pharaoh%27s_Dream.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tissot_Joseph_Interprets_Pharaoh%27s_Dream.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Joseph Interprets Pharaoh's Dream</i> c. 1896–1902. <a href="/wiki/James_Tissot" title="James Tissot">Jacques Joseph Tissot</a> (1836–1902).</div></div></div>
<p>Beginning in the late 19th century, Austrian neurologist <a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">Sigmund Freud</a>, founder of <a href="/wiki/Psychoanalysis" title="Psychoanalysis">psychoanalysis</a>, theorized that dreams reflect the dreamer's unconscious mind and specifically that dream content is shaped by unconscious wish fulfillment. He argued that important unconscious desires often relate to early childhood memories and experiences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freud1_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Freud1-7">[7]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carl_Jung" title="Carl Jung">Carl Jung</a> and others expanded on Freud's idea that dream content reflects the dreamer's unconscious desires.
</p><p>Dream interpretation can be a result of subjective ideas and experiences. One study found that most people believe that "their dreams reveal meaningful hidden truths".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-69">[69]</a></sup> The researchers surveyed students in the United States, South Korea, and India, and found that 74% of Indians, 65% of South Koreans and 56% of Americans believed their dream content provided them with meaningful insight into their unconscious beliefs and desires. This Freudian view of dreaming was believed significantly more than theories of dreaming that attribute dream content to memory consolidation, problem-solving, or as a byproduct of unrelated brain activity. The same study found that people attribute more importance to dream content than to similar thought content that occurs while they are awake. Americans were more likely to report that they would miss their flight if they dreamt of their plane crashing than if they thought of their plane crashing the night before flying (while awake), and that they would be as likely to miss their flight if they dreamt of their plane crashing the night before their flight as if there was an actual plane crash on the route they intended to take. Participants in the study were more likely to perceive dreams to be meaningful when the content of dreams was in accordance with their beliefs and desires while awake. They were more likely to view a positive dream about a friend to be meaningful than a positive dream about someone they disliked, for example, and were more likely to view a negative dream about a person they disliked as meaningful than a negative dream about a person they liked.
</p><p>According to surveys, it is common for people to feel their dreams are predicting subsequent life events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hines_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-hines-70">[70]</a></sup> Psychologists have explained these experiences in terms of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_memory_biases" title="List of memory biases">memory biases</a>, namely a selective memory for accurate predictions and distorted memory so that dreams are retrospectively fitted onto life experiences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hines_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-hines-70">[70]</a></sup> The multi-faceted nature of dreams makes it easy to find connections between dream content and real events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> The term "veridical dream" has been used to indicate dreams that reveal or contain truths not yet known to the dreamer, whether future events or secrets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>In one experiment, subjects were asked to write down their dreams in a diary. This prevented the selective memory effect, and the dreams no longer seemed accurate about the future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Another experiment gave subjects a fake diary of a student with apparently precognitive dreams. This diary described events from the person's life, as well as some predictive dreams and some non-predictive dreams. When subjects were asked to recall the dreams they had read, they remembered more of the successful predictions than unsuccessful ones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Images_and_literature">Images and literature</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dream_art" title="Dream art">Dream art</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Dream_world_(plot_device)" title="Dream world (plot device)">Dream world (plot device)</a></div>
<p>Graphic artists, writers and filmmakers all have found dreams to offer a rich vein for creative expression. In the West, artists' depictions of dreams in Renaissance and Baroque art often were related to Biblical narrative. Especially preferred by visual artists were the <a href="/wiki/Jacob%27s_Ladder" title="Jacob's Ladder">Jacob's Ladder</a> dream in Genesis and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Joseph%27s_dreams" title="St. Joseph's dreams">St. Joseph's dreams</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Matthew" title="Gospel of Matthew">Gospel according to Matthew</a>.
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 205px"><div style="width: 205px">

<p>Many later graphic artists have depicted dreams, including Japanese <a href="/wiki/Woodblock_printing_in_Japan" title="Woodblock printing in Japan">woodblock</a> artist <a href="/wiki/The_Dream_of_the_Fisherman%27s_Wife" title="The Dream of the Fisherman's Wife">Hokusai</a> (1760–1849) and Western European painters <a href="/wiki/The_Dream_(Rousseau_painting)" title="The Dream (Rousseau painting)">Rousseau</a> (1844–1910), <a href="/wiki/Le_R%C3%AAve_(Picasso)" title="Le Rêve (Picasso)">Picasso</a> (1881–1973), and <a href="/wiki/Dream_Caused_by_the_Flight_of_a_Bee_Around_a_Pomegranate_a_Second_Before_Awakening" title="Dream Caused by the Flight of a Bee Around a Pomegranate a Second Before Awakening">Dali</a> (1904–1989).
</p><p>In literature, dream frames were frequently used in medieval allegory to justify the narrative; <i><a href="/wiki/The_Book_of_the_Duchess" title="The Book of the Duchess">The Book of the Duchess</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> and <i>The Vision Concerning <a href="/wiki/Piers_Plowman" title="Piers Plowman">Piers Plowman</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> are two such <a href="/wiki/Dream_vision" title="Dream vision">dream visions</a>. Even before them, in antiquity, the same device had been used by <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lucian_of_Samosata" title="Lucian of Samosata">Lucian of Samosata</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alice_par_John_Tenniel_24.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="548" data-file-width="846" decoding="async" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Alice_par_John_Tenniel_24.png/200px-Alice_par_John_Tenniel_24.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Alice_par_John_Tenniel_24.png/300px-Alice_par_John_Tenniel_24.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Alice_par_John_Tenniel_24.png/400px-Alice_par_John_Tenniel_24.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alice_par_John_Tenniel_24.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The cheshire cat</i>, <a href="/wiki/John_Tenniel" title="John Tenniel">John Tenniel</a> (1820–1914), illustration in <i>Alice's Adventures in Wonderland</i>, 1866 edition.</div></div></div>
<p>Dreams have also featured in <a href="/wiki/Fantasy" title="Fantasy">fantasy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Speculative_fiction" title="Speculative fiction">speculative fiction</a> since the 19th century. One of the best-known dream worlds is <a href="/wiki/Wonderland_(fictional_country)" title="Wonderland (fictional country)">Wonderland</a> from <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Carroll" title="Lewis Carroll">Lewis Carroll</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Alice%27s_Adventures_in_Wonderland" title="Alice's Adventures in Wonderland">Alice's Adventures in Wonderland</a></i>, as well as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Looking-Glass_Land" title="Looking-Glass Land">Looking-Glass Land</a> from its sequel, <i><a href="/wiki/Through_the_Looking-Glass" title="Through the Looking-Glass">Through the Looking-Glass</a></i>. Unlike many dream worlds, Carroll's logic is like that of actual dreams, with transitions and flexible causality.
</p><p>Other fictional dream worlds include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dreamlands" title="Dreamlands">Dreamlands</a> of <a href="/wiki/H._P._Lovecraft" title="H. P. Lovecraft">H. P. Lovecraft</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Dream_Cycle" title="Dream Cycle">Dream Cycle</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Neverending_Story" title="The Neverending Story">The Neverending Story</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> world of Fantastica, which includes places like the Desert of Lost Dreams, the Sea of Possibilities and the Swamps of Sadness. Dreamworlds, shared hallucinations and other alternate realities feature in a number of works by <a href="/wiki/Philip_K._Dick" title="Philip K. Dick">Philip K. Dick</a>, such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Three_Stigmata_of_Palmer_Eldritch" title="The Three Stigmata of Palmer Eldritch">The Three Stigmata of Palmer Eldritch</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Ubik" title="Ubik">Ubik</a></i>. Similar themes were explored by <a href="/wiki/Jorge_Luis_Borges" title="Jorge Luis Borges">Jorge Luis Borges</a>, for instance in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Circular_Ruins" title="The Circular Ruins">The Circular Ruins</a></i>.
</p><p>Modern <a href="/wiki/Popular_culture" title="Popular culture">popular culture</a> often conceives of dreams, as did Freud, as expressions of the dreamer's deepest fears and desires.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Van_Riper_56_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Van_Riper_56-79">[79]</a></sup> In speculative fiction, the line between dreams and reality may be blurred even more in service to the story.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Van_Riper_57_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Van_Riper_57-80">[80]</a></sup> Dreams may be psychically invaded or manipulated (<i><a href="/wiki/Dreamscape_(1984_film)" title="Dreamscape (1984 film)">Dreamscape</a></i>, 1984; the <i><a href="/wiki/A_Nightmare_on_Elm_Street_(franchise)" title="A Nightmare on Elm Street (franchise)">Nightmare on Elm Street</a></i> films, 1984–2010; <i><a href="/wiki/Inception" title="Inception">Inception</a></i>, 2010) or even come literally true (as in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lathe_of_Heaven" title="The Lathe of Heaven">The Lathe of Heaven</a></i>, 1971).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Van_Riper_56_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-Van_Riper_56-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Lucidity">Lucidity</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lucid_dream" title="Lucid dream">Lucid dream</a></div>
<p>Lucid dreaming is the conscious perception of one's state while dreaming. In this state the dreamer may often have some degree of control over their own actions within the dream or even the characters and the environment of the dream. Dream control has been reported to improve with practiced deliberate lucid dreaming, but the ability to control aspects of the dream is not necessary for a dream to qualify as "lucid"—a lucid dream is any dream during which the dreamer knows they are dreaming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> The occurrence of lucid dreaming has been scientifically verified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watanabe2003_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Watanabe2003-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>"<a href="/wiki/Oneironautics" title="Oneironautics">Oneironaut</a>" is a term sometimes used for those who lucidly dream.
</p><p>In 1975, psychologist Keith Hearne successfully recorded a communication from a dreamer experiencing a lucid dream. On April 12, 1975, after agreeing to move his eyes left and right upon becoming lucid, the subject and Hearne's co-author on the resulting article, Alan Worsley, successfully carried out this task.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lucid_dream_communication_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lucid_dream_communication-83">[83]</a></sup> Years later, psychophysiologist <a href="/wiki/Stephen_LaBerge" title="Stephen LaBerge">Stephen LaBerge</a> conducted similar work including:
</p>
<ul><li>Using eye signals to map the subjective sense of time in dreams.</li>
<li>Comparing the electrical activity of the brain while singing awake and while dreaming.</li>
<li>Studies comparing in-dream sex, arousal, and orgasm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Communication between two dreamers has also been documented. The processes involved included <a href="/wiki/Electroencephalography" title="Electroencephalography">EEG</a> monitoring, ocular signaling, incorporation of reality in the form of red light stimuli and a coordinating website. The website tracked when both dreamers were dreaming and sent the stimulus to one of the dreamers where it was incorporated into the dream. This dreamer, upon becoming lucid, signaled with eye movements; this was detected by the website whereupon the stimulus was sent to the second dreamer, invoking incorporation into that dreamer's dream.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recollection">Recollection</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Dream_diary" title="Dream diary">Dream diary</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Riepenhausen_Raphael%27s_dream.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="495" data-file-width="549" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Riepenhausen_Raphael%27s_dream.jpg/250px-Riepenhausen_Raphael%27s_dream.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Riepenhausen_Raphael%27s_dream.jpg/375px-Riepenhausen_Raphael%27s_dream.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Riepenhausen_Raphael%27s_dream.jpg/500px-Riepenhausen_Raphael%27s_dream.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Riepenhausen_Raphael%27s_dream.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Raphael's dream</i> (1821). <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Riepenhausen" title="Johannes Riepenhausen">Johannes Riepenhausen</a> and Franz Riepenhausen.</div></div></div>
<p>The recollection of dreams is extremely unreliable, though it is a skill that can be trained. Dreams can usually be recalled if a person is awakened while dreaming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-npr_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-npr-86">[86]</a></sup> Women tend to have more frequent dream recall than men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-npr_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-npr-86">[86]</a></sup> Dreams that are difficult to recall may be characterized by relatively little <a href="/wiki/Affect_(psychology)" title="Affect (psychology)">affect</a>, and factors such as <a href="/wiki/Salience_(neuroscience)" title="Salience (neuroscience)">salience</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arousal" title="Arousal">arousal</a>, and interference play a role in dream recall. Often, a dream may be recalled upon viewing or hearing a random trigger or stimulus. The <a href="/wiki/Graded_Salience_Hypothesis" title="Graded Salience Hypothesis">salience hypothesis</a> proposes that dream content that is salient, that is, novel, intense, or unusual, is more easily remembered. There is considerable evidence that vivid, intense, or unusual dream content is more frequently recalled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-87">[87]</a></sup> A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dream_journal" title="Dream journal">dream journal</a> can be used to assist dream recall, for personal interest or <a href="/wiki/Psychotherapy" title="Psychotherapy">psychotherapy</a> purposes.
</p><p>Adults report remembering around two dreams per week, on average.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Unless a dream is particularly vivid and if one wakes during or immediately after it, the content of the dream is typically not remembered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Recording or reconstructing dreams may one day assist with dream recall. Using the permitted non-invasive technologies, <a href="/wiki/Functional_magnetic_resonance_imaging" title="Functional magnetic resonance imaging">functional magnetic resonance imaging</a> (fMRI) and <a href="/wiki/Electromyography" title="Electromyography">electromyography</a> (EMG), researchers have been able to identify basic dream imagery,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> dream speech activity<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> and dream motor behavior (such as walking and hand movements).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>In line with the salience hypothesis, there is considerable evidence that people who have more vivid, intense or unusual dreams show better recall. There is evidence that continuity of consciousness is related to recall. Specifically, people who have vivid and unusual experiences during the day tend to have more memorable dream content and hence better dream recall. People who score high on measures of personality traits associated with creativity, imagination, and fantasy, such as <a href="/wiki/Openness_to_experience" title="Openness to experience">openness to experience</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daydream" title="Daydream">daydreaming</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fantasy_prone_personality" title="Fantasy prone personality">fantasy proneness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Absorption_(psychology)" title="Absorption (psychology)">absorption</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hypnotic_susceptibility" title="Hypnotic susceptibility">hypnotic susceptibility</a>, tend to show more frequent dream recall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-87">[87]</a></sup> There is also evidence for continuity between the bizarre aspects of dreaming and waking experience. That is, people who report more bizarre experiences during the day, such as people high in <a href="/wiki/Schizotypy" title="Schizotypy">schizotypy</a> (psychosis proneness), have more frequent dream recall and also report more frequent <a href="/wiki/Nightmare" title="Nightmare">nightmares</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson_87-2"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Miscellany">Miscellany</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Illusion_of_reality">Illusion of reality</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dream_argument" title="Dream argument">Dream argument</a></div>
<p>Some philosophers have proposed that what we think of as the "real world" could be or is an illusion (an idea known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skeptical_hypothesis" title="Skeptical hypothesis">skeptical hypothesis</a> about <a href="/wiki/Ontology" title="Ontology">ontology</a>). The first recorded mention of the idea was in the 4th century BCE by <a href="/wiki/Zhuang_Zhou" title="Zhuang Zhou">Zhuangzi</a>, and in Eastern philosophy, the problem has been named the "<a href='/wiki/Zhuangzi_(book)#"The_Butterfly_Dream"' title="Zhuangzi (book)">Zhuangzi Paradox</a>."
</p>
<blockquote><p>He who dreams of drinking wine may weep when morning comes; he who dreams of weeping may in the morning go off to hunt. While he is dreaming he does not know it is a dream, and in his dream he may even try to interpret a dream. Only after he wakes does he know it was a dream. And someday there will be a great awakening when we know that this is all a great dream. Yet the stupid believe they are awake, busily and brightly assuming they understand things, calling this man ruler, that one herdsman—how dense! Confucius and you are both dreaming! And when I say you are dreaming, I am dreaming, too. Words like these will be labeled the Supreme Swindle. Yet, after ten thousand generations, a great sage may appear who will know their meaning, and it will still be as though he appeared with astonishing speed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>The idea also is discussed in Hindu and Buddhist writings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> It was formally introduced to Western philosophy by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Descartes" title="Descartes">Descartes</a> in the 17th century in his <i><a href="/wiki/Meditations_on_First_Philosophy" title="Meditations on First Philosophy">Meditations on First Philosophy</a></i>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Absent-minded_transgression">Absent-minded transgression</span></h3>
<p>Dreams of absent-minded transgression (DAMT) are dreams wherein the dreamer absent-mindedly performs an action that he or she has been trying to stop (one classic example is of a quitting smoker having dreams of lighting a cigarette). Subjects who have had DAMT have reported waking with intense feelings of <a href="/wiki/Guilt_(emotion)" title="Guilt (emotion)">guilt</a>. One study found a positive association between having these dreams and successfully stopping the behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Daydreams">Daydreams</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Daydream" title="Daydream">Daydream</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Joseph_Noel_Paton_-_Dante_Meditating.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="538" decoding="async" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Joseph_Noel_Paton_-_Dante_Meditating.jpg/200px-Joseph_Noel_Paton_-_Dante_Meditating.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Joseph_Noel_Paton_-_Dante_Meditating.jpg/300px-Joseph_Noel_Paton_-_Dante_Meditating.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Joseph_Noel_Paton_-_Dante_Meditating.jpg/400px-Joseph_Noel_Paton_-_Dante_Meditating.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Joseph_Noel_Paton_-_Dante_Meditating.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Dante Meditating</i>, 1852, by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Noel_Paton" title="Joseph Noel Paton">Joseph Noel Paton</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>A daydream is a visionary <a href="/wiki/Fantasy_(psychology)" title="Fantasy (psychology)">fantasy</a>, especially one of happy, pleasant thoughts, hopes or ambitions, imagined as coming to pass, and experienced while awake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Klinger_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Klinger-98">[98]</a></sup> There are many different types of daydreams, and there is no consistent definition amongst psychologists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Klinger_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-Klinger-98">[98]</a></sup> The general public also uses the term for a broad variety of experiences. Research by Harvard psychologist <a href="/wiki/Deirdre_Barrett" title="Deirdre Barrett">Deirdre Barrett</a> has found that people who experience vivid dreamlike <a href="/wiki/Mental_image" title="Mental image">mental images</a> reserve the word for these, whereas many other people refer to milder imagery, realistic future planning, review of memories or just "spacing out"—i.e. one's mind going relatively blank—when they talk about "daydreaming".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>While daydreaming has long been derided as a lazy, non-productive pastime, it is now commonly acknowledged that daydreaming can be constructive in some contexts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> There are numerous examples of people in creative or artistic careers, such as composers, novelists and filmmakers, developing new ideas through daydreaming. Similarly, research scientists, mathematicians and physicists have developed new ideas by daydreaming about their subject areas.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hallucination">Hallucination</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hallucination" title="Hallucination">Hallucination</a></div>
<p>A hallucination, in the broadest sense of the word, is a <a href="/wiki/Perception" title="Perception">perception</a> in the absence of a <a href="/wiki/Stimulus_(physiology)" title="Stimulus (physiology)">stimulus</a>. In a stricter sense, hallucinations are perceptions in a conscious and awake state, in the absence of external stimuli, and have qualities of real perception, in that they are vivid, substantial, and located in external objective space. The latter definition distinguishes hallucinations from the related phenomena of dreaming, which does not involve wakefulness.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nightmare">Nightmare</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nightmare" title="Nightmare">Nightmare</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_perturbed_young_woman_fast_asleep_Wellcome_V0016638.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2528" data-file-width="3084" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/A_perturbed_young_woman_fast_asleep_Wellcome_V0016638.jpg/200px-A_perturbed_young_woman_fast_asleep_Wellcome_V0016638.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/A_perturbed_young_woman_fast_asleep_Wellcome_V0016638.jpg/300px-A_perturbed_young_woman_fast_asleep_Wellcome_V0016638.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/A_perturbed_young_woman_fast_asleep_Wellcome_V0016638.jpg/400px-A_perturbed_young_woman_fast_asleep_Wellcome_V0016638.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_perturbed_young_woman_fast_asleep_Wellcome_V0016638.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Woman having a nightmare. Jean-Pierre Simon (1764–1810 or 1813).</div></div></div>
<p>A nightmare is an unpleasant dream that can cause a strong negative emotional response from the mind, typically <a href="/wiki/Fear" title="Fear">fear</a> or <a href="/wiki/Horror_and_terror" title="Horror and terror">horror</a>, but also <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Despair" title="Despair">despair</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anxiety" title="Anxiety">anxiety</a> and great <a href="/wiki/Sadness" title="Sadness">sadness</a>. The dream may contain situations of danger, discomfort, psychological or physical terror. Sufferers usually awaken in a state of distress and may be unable to return to <a href="/wiki/Sleep" title="Sleep">sleep</a> for a prolonged period of time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Night_terror">Night terror</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Night_terror" title="Night terror">Night terror</a></div>
<p>A night terror, also known as a sleep terror or <i>pavor nocturnus</i>, is a <a href="/wiki/Parasomnia" title="Parasomnia">parasomnia</a> <a href="/wiki/Sleep_disorder" title="Sleep disorder">disorder</a> that predominantly affects children, causing feelings of terror or dread. Night terrors should not be confused with <a href="/wiki/Nightmare" title="Nightmare">nightmares</a>, which are bad dreams that cause the feeling of horror or fear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="D.C3.A9j.C3.A0_vu"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Déjà_vu">Déjà vu</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A9j%C3%A0_vu" title="Déjà vu">Déjà vu</a></div>
<p>One theory of déjà vu attributes the feeling of having previously seen or experienced something to having dreamed about a similar situation or place, and forgetting about it until one seems to be mysteriously reminded of the situation or the place while awake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-51"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-51">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFLévy-Bruhl1923"><a href="/wiki/Lucien_L%C3%A9vy-Bruhl" title="Lucien Lévy-Bruhl">Lévy-Bruhl, Lucien</a> (1923). <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/primitivementali00levy_0/page/98/mode/2up?ref=ol&amp;view=theater" rel="nofollow">"Chapter III Dreams"</a>. <i>Primitive Mentality</i>. Translated by Lilian A. Clare. New York: Macmillan. p. 98. <q>...[I]n dreams,...man passes from the one world to the other without being aware of it. Such is in fact the ordinary idea of the dream to primitive peoples. The "soul" leaves its tenement for the time being. It frequently goes very far away; it communes with spirits or with ghosts. At the moment of awakening it returns to take its place in the body once more.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Chapter+III+Dreams&amp;rft.btitle=Primitive+Mentality&amp;rft.place=New+York&amp;rft.pages=98&amp;rft.pub=Macmillan&amp;rft.date=1923&amp;rft.aulast=L%C3%A9vy-Bruhl&amp;rft.aufirst=Lucien&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Farchive.org%2Fdetails%2Fprimitivementali00levy_0%2Fpage%2F98%2Fmode%2F2up%3Fref%3Dol%26view%3Dtheater&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADream"></span></span>
